<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas vehicles are popular in regions or countries where natural gas is abundant and where the government chooses to price CNG lower than gasoline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-4">[4]</a></sup> The use of natural gas began in the <a href="/wiki/Po_Valley" title="Po Valley">Po River Valley</a> of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> in the 1930s, followed by <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> in the 1980s, though its use has declined there. At the peak of New Zealand's natural gas use, 10% of the nation's cars were converted, around 110,000 vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-4">[4]</a></sup> In the United States CNG powered buses are the favorite choice of several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_transit" title="Public transit">public transit</a> agencies, with a fleet of more than 114,000 vehicles, mostly buses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenCar_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-GreenCar-61">[61]</a></sup> India, Australia, Argentina, and Germany also have widespread use of natural gas-powered buses in their public transportation fleets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brescia_Trasporti_Iveco_CityClass_632_via_Sardegna_20120828.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Brescia_Trasporti_Iveco_CityClass_632_via_Sardegna_20120828.JPG/220px-Brescia_Trasporti_Iveco_CityClass_632_via_Sardegna_20120828.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Brescia_Trasporti_Iveco_CityClass_632_via_Sardegna_20120828.JPG/330px-Brescia_Trasporti_Iveco_CityClass_632_via_Sardegna_20120828.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Brescia_Trasporti_Iveco_CityClass_632_via_Sardegna_20120828.JPG/440px-Brescia_Trasporti_Iveco_CityClass_632_via_Sardegna_20120828.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brescia_Trasporti_Iveco_CityClass_632_via_Sardegna_20120828.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CNG-powered bus in Italy</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CNG-powered_buses_in_Horlivka,_Ukraine.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1019" data-file-width="1261" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/CNG-powered_buses_in_Horlivka%2C_Ukraine.tif/lossy-page1-220px-CNG-powered_buses_in_Horlivka%2C_Ukraine.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/CNG-powered_buses_in_Horlivka%2C_Ukraine.tif/lossy-page1-330px-CNG-powered_buses_in_Horlivka%2C_Ukraine.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/CNG-powered_buses_in_Horlivka%2C_Ukraine.tif/lossy-page1-440px-CNG-powered_buses_in_Horlivka%2C_Ukraine.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CNG-powered_buses_in_Horlivka,_Ukraine.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CNG-powered buses in <a href="/wiki/Horlivka" title="Horlivka">Horlivka</a>, eastern  Ukraine</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Germany hit the milestone of 900 CNG filling stations nationwide in December 2011. Gibgas, an independent consumer group, estimates that 21% of all CNG filling stations in the country offer a natural gas/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomethane" title="Biomethane">biomethane</a> mix to varying ratios, and 38 stations offer pure biomethane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> uses natural gas buses for public transport in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>.
Also the Public Gas Company (DEPA) has a network of 11 stations (as of 2017), under brand "Fisikon", and plans more stations in next 5 years.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bus_%C3%89ireann" title="Bus Éireann">Bus Éireann</a> Introduced the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NGV" title="NGV">NGV</a> on 17 July 2012. It will operate on the 216 city centre to Mount Oval, Rochestown, route until mid-August on a trial being undertaken in partnership with <a href="/wiki/Ervia" title="Ervia">Ervia</a>. The Eco-city bus is made by <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Natural gas traction is quite popular in Italy, due to the existence of a capillar distribution network for industrial use since the late 50s and a traditionally high retail price for petrol. As of April 2012 there were about 1173 filling stations, mainly located in the northern regions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> while the fleet reached 730,000 CNG vehicles at the end of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ukraine">Ukraine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ukraine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ukraine’s first compressed natural gas refueling station (CNGS) was commissioned in 1937. Today, there is a well-developed CNGS network across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Many buses were converted to run on CNG during the 1990s, primarily for economic reasons. The retrofitted cylinders are often visible atop the vehicle's roof and/or underneath the body. Despite their age, these buses remain in service and continue to provide reliable public transport combined with the environmental benefits of CNG.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>CNG buses are beginning to be used in the UK, e.g. by <a href="/wiki/Reading_Buses" title="Reading Buses">Reading Buses</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the recent increase in natural gas production due to widespread use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fracking" title="Fracking">fracking</a> technology, many countries, including the United States and Canada, now can be self-sufficient. Canada is a substantial net exporter of natural gas, though the United States still has a net import of natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Natural gas prices have decreased dramatically in the past few years and are likely to decrease further as additional production comes on line. However, the EIA predicts that natural gas prices will start increasing in a few years as the most profitable natural gas reserves are used up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Natural gas prices have decreased from $13 per mmbtu (USD) in 2008 to $3 per mmbtu (USD) in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It is likely therefore that natural gas-powered vehicles will be increasingly cheaper to run relative to gasoline-powered vehicles. The issue is how to finance the purchase and installation of conversion kits. Some support may be available through the Department of Energy. Private initiatives which essentially lease the conversion equipment in exchange for slightly higher natural gas refueling can be self-financing and offer considerable advantages to liquidity strapped consumers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Street_Railway_510213_wide.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="1024" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Hamilton_Street_Railway_510213_wide.jpg/220px-Hamilton_Street_Railway_510213_wide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Hamilton_Street_Railway_510213_wide.jpg/330px-Hamilton_Street_Railway_510213_wide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Hamilton_Street_Railway_510213_wide.jpg/440px-Hamilton_Street_Railway_510213_wide.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Street_Railway_510213_wide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CNG-powered bus in <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ontario" title="Hamilton, Ontario">Hamilton, Ontario</a></div></div></div>
<p>Natural Gas has been used as a motor fuel in Canada for over 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> With assistance from federal and provincial research programs, demonstration projects, and NGV market deployment programs during the 1980s and 1990s, the population of light-duty NGVs grew to over 35,000 by the early 1990s. This assistance resulted in a significant adoption of natural gas transit buses as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iangv.org_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-iangv.org-71">[71]</a></sup> The NGV market started to decline after 1995, eventually reaching today’s vehicle population of about 12,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iangv.org_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-iangv.org-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>This figure includes 150 urban transit buses, 45 school buses, 9,450 light-duty cars and trucks, and 2,400 forklifts and ice-resurfacers. The total fuel use in all NGV markets in Canada was 1.9 petajoules (PJs) in 2007 (or 54.6 million litres of gasoline litres equivalent), down from 2.6 PJs in 1997. Public CNG refuelling stations have declined in quantity from 
134 in 1997 to 72 today. There are 22 in British Columbia, 12 in Alberta, 10 in Saskatchewan, 27 in 
Ontario, and 1 in Québec. There are only 12 private fleet stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metrobus_powered_with_CNG_5198_DCA_03_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="790" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Metrobus_powered_with_CNG_5198_DCA_03_2009.jpg/220px-Metrobus_powered_with_CNG_5198_DCA_03_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Metrobus_powered_with_CNG_5198_DCA_03_2009.jpg/330px-Metrobus_powered_with_CNG_5198_DCA_03_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Metrobus_powered_with_CNG_5198_DCA_03_2009.jpg/440px-Metrobus_powered_with_CNG_5198_DCA_03_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metrobus_powered_with_CNG_5198_DCA_03_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Buses powered with <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">CNG</a> are common in the United States</div></div></div>
<p>As of December 2009, the U.S. had a fleet of 114,270 <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG) vehicles, 147,030 vehicles running on <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a> (LPG), and 3,176 vehicles running on <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas" title="Liquefied natural gas">liquefied natural gas</a> (LNG).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USeDataBook_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-USeDataBook-73">[73]</a></sup> The NGV fleet is made up mostly of transit buses but there are also some government fleet cars and vans, as well as increasing number of corporate trucks replacing diesel versions, most notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waste_Management,_Inc" title="Waste Management, Inc">Waste Management, Inc</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Parcel_Service" title="United Parcel Service">UPS</a> trucks. As of 12-Dec-2013 Waste Management has a fleet of 2000 CNG Collection trucks; as of 12-Dec-2013 UPS has 2700 alternative fuel vehicles. As of February 2011, there were 873 CNG refueling sites, 2,589 LPG sites, and 40 LNG sites, led by <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> with 215 CNG refueling stations in operation, 228 LPG sites and 32 LNG sites. The number of refueling stations includes both public and private sites, and not all are available to the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USeDataBook_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-USeDataBook-73">[73]</a></sup> As of December 2010, the U.S. ranked 6th in the world in terms of number of NGV stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup> Currently there are 160,000 NGVs operating in the country.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The natural gas vehicle market is limited to fleet vehicles and other public use vehicles like minibuses in larger cities. However the state-owned bus company <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Red_de_Transporte_de_Pasajeros&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Red de Transporte de Pasajeros (page does not exist)">RTP</a> Of <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a> has purchased 30 <a href="/wiki/Hyundai" title="Hyundai">Hyundai</a> Super Aero City CNG-Propelled buses to integrate with the existing fleet as well as to introduce new routes within the city.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Posto_GNV_01_2009_485_CWB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Posto_GNV_01_2009_485_CWB.jpg/220px-Posto_GNV_01_2009_485_CWB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Posto_GNV_01_2009_485_CWB.jpg/330px-Posto_GNV_01_2009_485_CWB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Posto_GNV_01_2009_485_CWB.jpg/440px-Posto_GNV_01_2009_485_CWB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Posto_GNV_01_2009_485_CWB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CNG pumps at a Brazilian gasoline service station, <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_(state)" title="Paraná (state)">Paraná state</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SAO_09_2008_Fiat_Siena_TetraFuel_2_views_v1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1272" data-file-width="945" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/SAO_09_2008_Fiat_Siena_TetraFuel_2_views_v1.jpg/220px-SAO_09_2008_Fiat_Siena_TetraFuel_2_views_v1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/SAO_09_2008_Fiat_Siena_TetraFuel_2_views_v1.jpg/330px-SAO_09_2008_Fiat_Siena_TetraFuel_2_views_v1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/SAO_09_2008_Fiat_Siena_TetraFuel_2_views_v1.jpg/440px-SAO_09_2008_Fiat_Siena_TetraFuel_2_views_v1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SAO_09_2008_Fiat_Siena_TetraFuel_2_views_v1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Popular among <a href="/wiki/Taxicab" title="Taxicab">taxi</a> drivers, the Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Siena" title="Fiat Siena">Fiat Siena Tetrafuel</a> 1.4, is a <a href="/wiki/Multifuel" title="Multifuel">multifuel</a> car that runs as a <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel</a> on pure <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E20,_E25" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E20-E25 blend</a>, or pure ethanol (<a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E100" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E100</a>); or runs as a <a href="/wiki/Bi-fuel_vehicle" title="Bi-fuel vehicle">bi-fuel</a> with <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">natural gas (CNG)</a>. Below: the CNG storage tanks in the trunk.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview_2">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>CNG vehicles are common in South America, with a 35% share of the worldwide NGV fleet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup> where these vehicles are mainly used as <a href="/wiki/Taxicab" title="Taxicab">taxicabs</a> in main cities of Argentina and Brazil. Normally, standard gasoline vehicles are retrofitted in specialized shops, which involve installing the gas cylinder in the trunk and the CNG injection system and electronics.
</p><p>As of 2009 Argentina had 1,807,186 NGV's with 1,851 refueling stations across the nation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup> or 15% of all vehicles;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LatinGNV_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-LatinGNV-74">[74]</a></sup> and Brazil had 1,632,101 vehicles and 1,704 refueling stations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup> with a higher concentration in the cities of <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a> and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GNVNews_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-GNVNews-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LatinGNV_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-LatinGNV-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Colombia had an NGV fleet of 300,000 vehicles, and 460 refueling stations as of 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> has increased its fleet from 10,000 in 2003 to 121,908 units in 2009, with 128 refueling stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Peru had 81,024 NGVs and 94 fueling stations as 2009,.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup> In Peru, several factory-built CNVs have the tanks installed under the body of the vehicle, leaving the trunk free. Among the models built with this feature are the <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Multipla" title="Fiat Multipla">Fiat Multipla</a>, the new <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Panda" title="Fiat Panda">Fiat Panda</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Touran" title="Volkswagen Touran">Volkswagen Touran</a> Ecofuel, the <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Caddy" title="Volkswagen Caddy">Volkswagen Caddy</a> Ecofuel, and the Chevy Taxi. Right now, Peru has 224,035 NGVs.
</p><p>Other countries with significant NGV fleets are <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> (226,100) as of 2017 and <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> (15,000) as of 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latest_developments">Latest developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Latest developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GM_do_Brasil" title="GM do Brasil">GM do Brasil</a> introduced the MultiPower engine in August 2004 which was capable of using CNG, alcohol and gasoline as fuel. The GM engine has electronic fuel injection that automatically adjusts to any acceptable fuel configuration. This motor was used in the <a href="/wiki/Opel_Astra" title="Opel Astra">Chevrolet Astra</a> and was aimed at the taxi market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GNVNews_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-GNVNews-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006 the Brazilian subsidiary of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiat" title="Fiat">Fiat</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Siena" title="Fiat Siena">Fiat Siena Tetra fuel</a>, a four-fuel car developed under <a href="/wiki/Magneti_Marelli" title="Magneti Marelli">Magneti Marelli</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiat" title="Fiat">Fiat</a> Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TreeH_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-TreeH-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> This automobile can run on 100% ethanol (<a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E100" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E100</a>), <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E20,_E25" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E20 to E25</a> blend (Brazil's normal ethanol gasoline blend), pure gasoline (not available in Brazil), and natural gas, and switches from the gasoline-ethanol blend to CNG automatically, depending on the power required by road conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 2003 and with the commercial success of flex cars in Brazil, another existing option is to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrofit" title="Retrofit">retrofit</a> an ethanol <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel vehicle</a> to add a natural gas tank and the corresponding injection system. Some <a href="/wiki/Taxicab" title="Taxicab">taxicabs</a> in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, Brazil, run on this option, allowing the user to choose among three fuels (E25, E100 and CNG) according to current market prices at the pump. Vehicles with this adaptation are known in Brazil as <b>tri-fuel</b> cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> was the country with the second largest fleet of NGV with a total of 2.85 million by the end of 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Most of the public transportation fleet has been converted to CNG.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Also, in Pakistan and India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> there have been on-going (last several years now) series of CNG fuel shortages which periodically waxes and wanes, getting the fuel into a tank can be a major problem. In July 2011, petrol usage shot up 15% from the month before due to shortages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Pakistan also has reported that over 2,000 people have died in 2011 from CNG cylinder blasts, because of low quality of cylinders there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In 2012, the Pakistani government took the decision to gradually phase out CNG sector altogether beginning by banning any new conversions to CNG and banning the manufacturing of new NGV's. In addition the government plans to close down all refueling stations in the next 3 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CNG_filling_station,_Delhi.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/CNG_filling_station%2C_Delhi.JPG/220px-CNG_filling_station%2C_Delhi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/CNG_filling_station%2C_Delhi.JPG/330px-CNG_filling_station%2C_Delhi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/CNG_filling_station%2C_Delhi.JPG/440px-CNG_filling_station%2C_Delhi.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CNG_filling_station,_Delhi.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A CNG powered car being filled in a filling station in Delhi</div></div></div>
<p>In 1993, CNG had become available in <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, India's capital, though LPG is what really took off due to its inherently far lower capital costs. Compressed Natural Gas is a domestic energy produced in Western parts of India. In India, most CNG vehicles are dual fueled, which means they can run both on CNG and gasoline. This makes it very convenient and users can drive long distances without worrying about availability of natural gas (as long as gasoline is available). As of December 2010 India had 1,080,000 NGVs and 560 fueling stations, many of the older ones being LPG rather than CNG.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup> In addition, it is thought that more illegally converted LPG autos than legal ones ply the streets in India, some estimates are as high as 15 million "autos" (running the gamut of everything from LPG motored pedal bicycles to CNG buses)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, a lawyer filed a case with the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_India" title="Supreme Court of India">Supreme Court of India</a> under the Public Interest Litigation rule, which is part of the Constitution of India and enables any citizen to address directly the Supreme Court. The lawyer’s case was about the health risks caused by air pollution emitted from road vehicles. The Supreme Court decided that cars put into circulation after 1995 would have to run on unleaded fuel. By 1998, India was converted to 100% of unleaded fuel after the government ruled that diesel cars in India were restricted to 10,000 ppm after 1995. At the beginning of 2005, 10,300 CNG busses, 55,000 CNG three-wheelers taxis, 5,000 CNG minibuses, 10,000 CNG taxis and 10,000 CNG cars run on India’s roads (1982-2008 Product-Life Institute, Geneva). The Delhi Transport Corporation currently operates the world's largest fleet of CNG buses for public transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Currently India stands 3rd with 3.045 million NGVs.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By the end of 2015, Iran had the world's largest fleet of NGV at 3.5 million vehicles. The share of compressed natural gas in the national fuel basket is more than 23%. CNG consumption by Iran’s transportation sector is around 20 million cubic meters per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> There are 2,335 CNG stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The growth of NGV market in Iran has in large part been due to Iranian government intervention to decrease the society's dependence on gasoline. This governmental plan was implemented to reduce the effect of sanctions on Iran and make the nation's domestic market less dependent on imported gasoline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Iran has been manufacturing its own NGV's through local manufacturing using <a href="/wiki/IKCO_EF_Engines" title="IKCO EF Engines">dedicated CNG engines</a> which use gasoline only as a back up fuel. Also by 2012, Iranian manufacturers had the capacity to build 1.5 million CNG cylinders per year and therefore Iranian government has banned their imports to support the local manufacturers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In addition CNG in Iran costs the least compared to the rest of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In 2012, the Iranian government announced a plan to replace the traditional CNG cylinders with <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">Adsorbed</a> Natural Gas (ANG) cylinders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(December 2016)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:No.39.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="1920" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/No.39.jpg/220px-No.39.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/No.39.jpg/330px-No.39.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/No.39.jpg/440px-No.39.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:No.39.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A CNG powered <a href="/wiki/Hino_Motors" title="Hino Motors">Hino RU1JSSL</a> bus, operated by <a href="/wiki/Bangkok_Mass_Transit_Authority" title="Bangkok Mass Transit Authority">BMTA</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> has for over a 15 years run <a href="/wiki/Autogas" title="Autogas">autogas</a> taxi cabs in Bangkok,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thailand08_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thailand08-97">[97]</a></sup> though autos and buses had erroneously labelled NGV stickers on them, when in fact, were LPG fuelled.
</p><p>In view of a generous supply of natural gas but relying on imported oil, the Thailand government heavily promoted alternative fuels like LPG, natural gas and ethanol to replace gasoline beginning around 2003, yet NGV was very slow to take off due to cheaper LPG fuel, a pre-existing LPG fleet, and very low conversion cost of local LPG conversion shops as compared to factory installed CNG or conversion. A significant effort was taken when the state-controlled oil company <a href="/wiki/PTT_Public_Company_Limited" title="PTT Public Company Limited">PTT PCL</a> built a network of natural gas refueling stations. The cost of subsidy was estimated at US$150 million in 2008.
</p><p>As price of oil climbed rapidly, it was estimated more than 40,000 new cars and trucks powered by natural-gas were purchased in six months in 2008, including many buses. That year, about half of the taxi fleet in Bangkok used LPG, and were prodded to convert to CNG, with little success. Since 2008, there has been a government arm-twisting to switch from LPG to CNG, with a rollout of CNG stations near Bangkok around 2007 and then upcountry in 2010, at times replacing LPG stations. Operators of used vehicles have balked at the massive conversion cost (up to quadruple that of LPG in Thailand), especially given Thailand's strong ultra-competitive domestic LPG conversion industry, as well as retail CNG fuel cost (one and a half times). Thailand had some 700,000 LPG fueled vehicles, and 300,000 CNG fueled, with 1,000 LPG stations and 600 CNG as of 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Demand has increased 26% over 2011 for CNG in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> As of the end of 2012, Thailand has 1,014,000 LPG fueled vehicles, and consumed 606,000 tonnes in 2012 of LPG, while 483 stations serve up some 380,000 CNG vehicles.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> showing that LPG conversion continues to enjoy heavy favor over NGVs despite massive government push for CNG. CNG vehicles are more likely to be bought factory installed while LPG is likely to be an aftermarket conversion. LNG vehicles in Thailand are almost non-existent except for lorries.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taxi_in_Kuala_Lumpur_04.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Taxi_in_Kuala_Lumpur_04.JPG/220px-Taxi_in_Kuala_Lumpur_04.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Taxi_in_Kuala_Lumpur_04.JPG/330px-Taxi_in_Kuala_Lumpur_04.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Taxi_in_Kuala_Lumpur_04.JPG/440px-Taxi_in_Kuala_Lumpur_04.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taxi_in_Kuala_Lumpur_04.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>NGV Proton Iswara taxi in Malaysia</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, the use of <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> was originally introduced for taxicabs and airport limousines during the late-1990s, when new taxis were launched with NGV engines while taxicab operators were encouraged to send in existing taxis for full engine conversions, reducing their costs of operation. Any vehicle converted to use CNG is labelled with white rhombus "NGV" (Natural Gas Vehicle) tags, lending to the common use of "NGV" when referring to road vehicles with CNG engine. The practice of using CNG remained largely confined to taxicabs predominantly in the <a href="/wiki/Klang_Valley" title="Klang Valley">Klang Valley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penang" title="Penang">Penang</a> due to a lack of interest. No incentives were offered for those besides taxicab owners to use CNG engines, while government subsidies on petrol and diesel made conventional road vehicles cheaper to use in the eyes of the consumers. <a href="/wiki/Petronas" title="Petronas">Petronas</a>, Malaysia's state-owned oil company, also monopolises the provision of CNG to road users. As of  July 2008<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Petronas only operates about 150 CNG refueling stations, most of which are concentrated in the Klang Valley. At the same time, another 50 was expected by the end of 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MY_CNG_station_no_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-MY_CNG_station_no-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>As fuel subsidies were gradually removed in Malaysia starting June 5, 2008, the subsequent 41% price hike on petrol and diesel led to a 500% increase in the number of new CNG tanks installed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MY_rush_1_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-MY_rush_1-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MY_rush_2_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-MY_rush_2-103">[103]</a></sup> National car maker <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_(carmaker)" title="Proton (carmaker)">Proton</a> considered fitting its <a href="/wiki/Proton_Waja" title="Proton Waja">Waja</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proton_Saga" title="Proton Saga">Saga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proton_Persona" title="Proton Persona">Persona</a> models with CNG kits from Prins Autogassystemen by the end of 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MY_Potong_CNG_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-MY_Potong_CNG-104">[104]</a></sup> while a local distributor of locally assembled <a href="/wiki/Hyundai_Motor_Company" title="Hyundai Motor Company">Hyundai</a> cars offers new models with CNG kits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MY_Hyundai_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-MY_Hyundai-105">[105]</a></sup> Conversion centres, which also benefited from the rush for lower running costs, also perform partial conversions to existing road vehicles, allowing them to run on both petrol or diesel and CNG with a cost varying between <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_ringgit" title="Malaysian ringgit">RM</a>3,500 to RM5,000 for passenger cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MY_rush_1_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-MY_rush_1-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MY_CNG_conversion_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-MY_CNG_conversion-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Volvo_B10BLE_SBS_Transit_SBS2988J.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Volvo_B10BLE_SBS_Transit_SBS2988J.jpg/220px-Volvo_B10BLE_SBS_Transit_SBS2988J.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Volvo_B10BLE_SBS_Transit_SBS2988J.jpg/330px-Volvo_B10BLE_SBS_Transit_SBS2988J.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Volvo_B10BLE_SBS_Transit_SBS2988J.jpg/440px-Volvo_B10BLE_SBS_Transit_SBS2988J.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Volvo_B10BLE_SBS_Transit_SBS2988J.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A CNG powered <a href="/wiki/Volvo_B10BLE" title="Volvo B10BLE">Volvo B10BLE</a> bus, operated by <a href="/wiki/SBS_Transit" title="SBS Transit">SBS Transit</a> in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There were about 400 CNG-fueled vehicles in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> in mid-2007, of which about 110 are taxis operated by Smart Automobile. By February 2008, the number has risen 520 CNG vehicles, of which about half are taxis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-107">[107]</a></sup> All vehicles had to refuel at the sole CNG station operated by Sembcorp Gas and located on <a href="/wiki/Jurong_Island" title="Jurong Island">Jurong Island</a> until the opening of the first publicly accessible CNG station at <a href="/wiki/Mandai" title="Mandai">Mandai</a> in 2008, operated by Smart Automobile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The company plans to build another four stations by 2011, by which time the company projects to operate 3,000 to 4,000 CNG taxis, and with 10,000 CNG public and commercial vehicles of other types on Singapore's roads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Sembcorp Gas opened its second CNG station a week after the Mandai station at Jalan Buroh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>CNG is almost unheard of as a transport fuel before 2010 in the archipelago except in <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, where a very relatively minor number of vehicles, most notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transjakarta" title="Transjakarta">Transjakarta</a> buses, use the fuel. However, since 2010 there has been a government emphasis to push usage of CNG not only for vehicle fuel, but also for domestic consumption over wood burning (which can produce deadly methanol) and kerosene.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia">East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>China had 450,000 NGV's and 870 refueling stations as of 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IANGV_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-IANGV-2">[2]</a></sup> China in 2012 has 1 million NGVs on the roads, 3 million forecast for 2015, with over 2000 stations (both CNG and LPG), with plans for 12,000 by 2020. Currently China leads the World with 5 million NGVs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> China also has lot of vehicles running of Petrol blended with Methanol as M15 and M85. 
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Korea">South Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: South Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For the purpose of improving air quality in the metropolitan area of <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, CNG buses were first introduced in July, 1997. By 2014, all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seoul_buses" title="Seoul buses">Seoul buses</a> were operating on CNG.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  Hyundai motor developed a CNG hybrid bus with 34.5% more-fuel efficiency and 30% lower pollution compared to CNG buses.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As a result, Seoul city government plans to change to CNG hybrid buses for 2,235 low-bed disabled-friendly CNG bus in Seoul.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>CNG buses are operation in other major South Korean cities like Busan, Daegu, Daejeon, Gwangju and Incheon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Motorsport">Motorsport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Motorsport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Advert" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>contains content that is written like <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_a_soapbox_or_means_of_promotion" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">an advertisement</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas_vehicle&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by removing <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Spam" title="Wikipedia:Spam">promotional content</a> and inappropriate <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:External_links" title="Wikipedia:External links">external links</a>, and by adding encyclopedic content written from a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutral point of view</a>.</span> <small><i>(September 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A new category of motorcar racing unites teams which compete with cars powered by natural gas, to demonstrate the effectiveness of natural gas as an alternative fuel. ECOMOTORI (magazine) Racing Team<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The magazine's team participates in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FIA_Alternative_Energies_Cup" title="FIA Alternative Energies Cup">FIA Alternative Energies Cup</a> and the talian <a class="extiw" href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Campionato_Italiano_CSAI_Energie_Alternative" title="it:Campionato Italiano CSAI Energie Alternative">ACI/CSAI Alternative Energies Championship</a>. In 2012, the team, led by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nicola_Ventura&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nicola Ventura (page does not exist)">Nicola Ventura</a>, competes with a Fiat 500 Abarth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> modified to run on natural gas with a Cavagna/Bigas fuel conversion kit and thus renamed "500 EcoAbarth". The driver is <a href="/wiki/Massimo_Liverani" title="Massimo Liverani">Massimo Liverani</a> while in the role of navigator, alternate Valeria Strada, Alessandro Talmelli and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fulvio_Ciervo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fulvio Ciervo (page does not exist)">Fulvio Ciervo</a>. On October 14, 2012, at the end of the 7th Ecorally San Marino-Vatican with 3 wins and a second place (out of 4 races),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> the Team also won the Italian CSAI Alternative Energy Pilots and Navigators titles. On 28 October 2012, after having raced in 7 European countries, collecting 3 wins, 2 second places and additional points, the team won the FIA Alternative Energies Drivers and Constructors world titles. For the first time ever, a car powered by methane won an FIA world title. In 2013, the team raced in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FIA_Alternative_Energies_Cup" title="FIA Alternative Energies Cup">FIA Alternative Energies Cup</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Campionato_Italiano_CSAI_Energie_Alternative" title="it:Campionato Italiano CSAI Energie Alternative">CSAI</a> Championships. The "500 EcoAbarth" of Ecomotori.net dominated the season, winning 5 of 5 titles. Thanks to the work of the team, the Abarth once again won a constructors' title since its last win 46 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
